Panarici apostema is the condition where there's an escape of pus from pressure wounds on either foot or hand due to infection and inflammation at joint areas that are left open without proper dressing, leading to a risk of developing gas inside the abscessed tissue. The treatment for this medical situation requires immediate attention as it involves debriding infected necrotic (dead) material from wounds along with antibiotics if necessary and specialized care in managing deep-tissue pus accumulation, which can result in further complications like osteomyelitis when the bone becomes involved. This condition typically requires extended hospital stays or outpatient follow-up to prevent recurrence of gas formation within abscessed tissues that are accompanied by a foul smell and dusky coloration indicative of poor healing conditions, underscoring urgent medical intervention is critical for these patients due to the potential severity.

When the bone heals after being broken, and according to Roman beliefs regarding body fluids, they treat the delicate flesh of an arm or leg as follows: once it is reset by a surgeon within its natural joint positioning. The text seems archaic but appears to touch on practices that intertwine medical treatment with cultural
When a bone fracture is treated, according to Roman views on the body'curvature of fluids: once it has been reset by an orthopedic surgeon in accordance with its natural joint alignment. The fluid aspect relates metaphorically rather than literally since Romans considered such treatments based more on humoral balance and less strictly scientific principles, considering factors like too much sun leading to excessive inflammation (inflammatory 'fluids'), overly tight muscles prohibiting proper blood flow ('strictures' in the fluids), unnecessary external padding or wraps contributing to congestion within limbs ('embroccaccio superflua’, referring here metaphorically to extra medical intervention which can lead to fluid stasis affecting healing - a concept not unaligned with later understandings of edema and improper wound care), or excessive movement during the critical phase of bone healing ('movement superfluente'), as well as episodes of restlessness in limbs after injury due to trauma (which could impede proper alignment, leading to suboptimal conditions for fracture repair). The Romans would have integrated these considerations with their beliefs about the four humors—blood, phlegm, yellow bile and black bile—into treatment practices.
The care for the fluids consists of draining excess humors from limbs and promoting gentle blood circulation within them until it is magnified in flow throughout the body, while administering baths to soothe; providing bandages as well as letting air over parts with ailment. This text reflects an ancient understanding where balance among bodily fluids was key for health—a notion based on Greek and Roman humoral theories which have since been discredited but illustrate the historical context of medical practices intertwined with spiritual beliefs regarding body harmony
